

{0}------------------------------------------------

# Playing Tag with Okamoto-Schnorr: Three-Move Pairing-Free Blind Signatures from DDH

Rutchathon Chairattana-Apirom<sup>1</sup> [,](https://orcid.org/0009-0006-1990-1329) Michael Reichle<sup>2</sup> [,](https://orcid.org/0000-0002-3498-5472) and Stefano Tessaro[1](https://orcid.org/0000-0002-3751-8546)

> <sup>1</sup> Paul G. Allen School of Computer Science & Engineering University of Washington, Seattle, US {rchairat,tessaro}@cs.washington.edu <sup>2</sup> Department of Computer Science ETH Zurich, Switzerland michael.reichle@inf.ethz.ch

Abstract. This paper presents the first blind signature scheme in a pairing-free group with the following properties: (1) the signing protocol consists of only three moves; (2) the proof of one-more unforgeability relies solely on the Decisional Diffie-Hellman (DDH) assumption in the Random Oracle Model (ROM); and (3) the construction makes only black-box use of the underlying group. This resolves a major open problem in the area, as all prior pairing-free blind signatures either additionally relied on the Algebraic Group Model (AGM) or required at least four moves. Moreover, a recent lower bound by Dietz et al. (ePrint, '26) shows that three moves are optimal for such constructions.

Both the communication complexity and the signature size in our scheme consist of a constant number of group elements. Our construction in fact achieves strong one-more unforgeability (which was not known for any of the recent AGM-free constructions requiring four moves), and we also present a partially blind variant. Furthermore, blindness is statistical (in the ROM). Our approach is based on a new construction paradigm that combines a conventional (yet, by itself, not fully secure) blind signature scheme (specifically, the blind Okamoto-Schnorr scheme) with a carefully crafted algebraic MAC.

{1}------------------------------------------------

{2}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-2-0"></span>1 Introduction

Blind signatures [\[Cha82\]](#page-51-0) enable a user to obtain a signature on a chosen message from a signer, without the signer learning neither the message being signed nor the resulting signature. They play a central role in privacy-preserving authentication. For example, they are widely used in industry proposals [\[HIP](#page-52-0)`21, [DGS](#page-51-1)`18, [CDVW24\]](#page-51-2) for anonymous authentication tokens, as well as in solutions for fraud-detection in privacy-preserving ad-click measurement [\[PCM,](#page-53-0) [HIJ](#page-52-1)`21, [Tru\]](#page-53-1). Other examples are iCloud Private Relay [\[App\]](#page-50-0) and Google One VPN Service [\[Goo\]](#page-52-2). Furthermore, blind signatures underlie several proposals for electronic cash [\[Cha82,](#page-51-0) [CFN90,](#page-51-3) [OO92\]](#page-53-2) and voting [\[FOO93\]](#page-52-3).

Pairing-free blind signatures. A very natural question, which has been studied over more than three decades, is whether blind signatures can be built in pairing-free groups, as many classical signatures, such as Schnorr signatures [\[Sch90\]](#page-53-3), EdDSA [\[BDL](#page-51-4)`12], and ECDSA [\[Ame05\]](#page-50-1) rely on such groups, and it is natural to expect blind versions of these (or related) signatures. This is also a practically relevant question since pairing-free elliptic curves are widely supported by cryptography libraries, in contrast to pairing-friendly curves. The latter, while enabling much simpler blind signatures (such as Blind BLS [\[Bol03\]](#page-51-5)), are not as widely supported and have less certain security guarantees (as they are subject to asymptotically faster discrete logarithm attacks in the target group). This problem is also theoretically interesting, as pairing-free groups often constitute the simplest algebraic structure enabling efficient public key cryptography.

For such schemes, a major challenge is achieving the standard security notion of one-more unforgeability (OMUF) [\[JLO97\]](#page-52-4), which is expected to hold against malicious users that interact with the signer in multiple concurrent sessions. Recently, Dietz et al. [\[DKT26\]](#page-51-6) established that a large class of constructions that can be described in a combination of the generic group model (GGM) [\[Sho97,](#page-53-4) [Mau05\]](#page-52-5) and random oracle model (ROM) [\[BR93\]](#page-51-7) require at least three moves between the signer and the user. This class of constructions captures exactly those constructions that make black-box use of the underlying group—these are generally more efficient. But if a blind signature requires more than a single round trip, then concurrent security is not equivalent to sequential security. This often leads to concurrent attacks on natural instantiations.

For example, the blind version of Schnorr signatures [\[CP93\]](#page-51-8) is easily compromised by concurrent attacks efficiently solving the ROS problem [\[BLL](#page-51-9)`21]. This attack extends to a number of other blind signatures based on linear identification, such as blind Okamoto-Schnorr signatures [\[Oka94\]](#page-53-5). At the same time, proofs of security for a small number of concurrent sessions (and hence not contradicting the attacks) have been given in the literature [\[PS00,](#page-53-6) [HKL19,](#page-52-6) [FPS20,](#page-52-7) [KLX22b,](#page-52-8) [KLX22a\]](#page-52-9).

Only recently, a number of efficient three-move blind signatures [\[FPS20,](#page-52-7) [KLRX22,](#page-52-10) [TZ22,](#page-53-7) [CKM](#page-51-10)`23] have been given, all with proofs of full OMUF security (i.e., no bounded number of sessions) assuming a combination of the Algebraic Group Model (AGM) [\[FKL18\]](#page-52-11) and the ROM. Despite this being substantial progress, there is much debate around the assurance we should infer from an AGM proof. Regardless of someone's position in this debate, it is certainly undesirable (and uncommon) if no solution without the AGM exists at all.

Avoiding the AGM. As a testament of the challenge of avoiding reliance on ideal models, Abe [\[Abe01\]](#page-50-2) proposed a three-move paring-free blind signature with a proof in the ROM (under DDH) which was later discovered to be flawed and could only be repaired in the GGM+ROM [\[OA03\]](#page-52-12), and later in the AGM+ROM [\[KLRX22\]](#page-52-10). Only very recently, the question of removing reliance on the AGM was fully resolved by a number of pairing-free blind signatures [\[CATZ24,](#page-51-11) [KRW24,](#page-52-13) [BHKR25,](#page-51-12) [KR25,](#page-52-14) [RR25,](#page-53-8) [KLR25\]](#page-52-15) with security proofs only relying on the ROM and standard assumptions in pairing-free groups. These constructions are summarized in Table [1,](#page-3-1) along with those proved secure in the AGM+ROM.

It is however noteworthy that without relying on the AGM, to date, no three-move construction is known. This leaves us with the following open question:

Can we design a three-move pairing-free blind signature in the ROM for which the security proof does not rely on the AGM?

This paper fully resolves this question by presenting the first three-move pairing-free blind signature for which OMUF is proved in the ROM under the DDH assumption. Furthermore, blindness is statistical (in

{3}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-3-1"></span>Table 1. Pairing-free blind signature schemes. We present constructions with full OMUF (i.e., there is no limit on the number of concurrent signing sessions) that make black-box use of the group. The schemes below (resp. above) the line rely on the ROM (resp. ROM+AGM). Our schemes from Section 4.1 (resp. Section 4.2) satisfy computational (resp. statistical) blindness. (†): These schemes satisfy a weaker one-more unforgeability notion.

the ROM). As stated above, by [DKT26], the round complexity of our construction is best possible, as it only makes generic use of the underlying group. Both communication and signatures consist of a constant number of group elements.

Additionally, we prove our construction to be one-more *strongly* unforgeable (OMSUF), and this was so far only known to be achievable by *five*-move constructions without the AGM. We also give a version of our construction which is partially blind, i.e., signing can also depend on a public input.

We give further details about our construction paradigm in the technical overview below.

ON NON-BLACK-BOX CONSTRUCTIONS. We stress that one can obtain three- (or even two-) move pairing-free constructions fairly generically by using Fischlin's construction [Fis06], in conjunction with non-black-box use of the group (e.g., by using generic NIZKs for statements that depend on the group). Recent work shows that the construction can in fact be instantiated (in the ROM) from a one-way function alone [CMV26]. However, these instantiations are generally much less efficient than what can be achieved by constructions using the group in a black-box way. Recently, Kastner et al. [KTZ26] presented a more efficient instantiation of this paradigm, although still relying on generic zero-knowledge. Their security proof also relies on the AGM and other non-standard assumptions.

#### <span id="page-3-0"></span>1.1 Technical Overview

Our starting point is the Okamoto-Schnorr blind signature [PS00], hereafter denoted BS<sub>os</sub>, based on the Okamoto-Schnorr identification scheme [Sch90, Oka93]. We give a brief recap. The scheme is parameterized by generators G and H of some group  $\mathbb G$  of prime-order p. Note that we employ additive notation for  $\mathbb G$ . The public key is  $W = w_0 G + w_1 H$  with secret key  $\mathbf w = (w_0, w_1) \leftarrow \mathbb Z_p^2$ . For notational convenience, we denote by  $\phi_{os}$  the linear map such that  $\phi_{os}(\mathbf w) = W$ . To sign a message  $\mu$ , the user engages in a 3-move protocol with the signer. Roughly, the signer-user interaction issues a  $\Sigma$ -protocol transcript  $\tau_{os} = (A_{os}, c_{os}, z_{os})$  for knowledge of  $\mathbf w$  to the user, while the user blinds the transcript  $\tau_{os}$  and compiles it into a Fiat-Shamir proof  $\widetilde{\tau}_{os} = (\widetilde{A}_{os}, \widetilde{c}_{os}, \widetilde{z}_{os})$ , where  $\widetilde{c}_{os} = H(W, \widetilde{A}_{os}, \mu)$ . In more detail, a signing session for message  $\mu$  proceeds as follows.

{4}------------------------------------------------

Move 1 (S  $\rightarrow$  U). The signer S samples  $r \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  and computes the nonce  $A_{os} \leftarrow \phi_{os}(r)$ . Then, it sends  $A_{os}$  to the user U.

Move 2 (U  $\rightarrow$  S). The user U blinds  $A_{os}$  into  $\widetilde{A}_{os}$ , computes  $\widetilde{c}_{os} \leftarrow \mathsf{H}(W, \widetilde{A}_{os}, \mu)$  and blinds  $\widetilde{c}_{os}$  into  $c_{os}$ . That is, the user samples  $\alpha \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  and  $\gamma \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p$ . Next, it sets  $\widetilde{A}_{os} \leftarrow A_{os} + \phi_{os}(\alpha) + \gamma W$  and  $c_{os} = \widetilde{c}_{os} - \gamma$ . Then, U sends the blinded challenge  $c_{os}$  to the signer S.

Move 3 (S  $\rightarrow$  U). The signer S sends the  $\Sigma$ -protocol response  $z_{os} \leftarrow c_{os} w + r$  to U.

**Derivation.** The user U blinds  $z_{os}$  into  $\widetilde{z}_{os} \leftarrow z_{os} + \alpha$  and sets  $\widetilde{\tau}_{os} = (\widetilde{A}_{os}, \widetilde{c}_{os}, \widetilde{z}_{os})$ . The derived signature is  $\sigma \leftarrow \widetilde{\tau}_{os}$ .

**Verification.** Given  $\sigma = (\widetilde{A}_{os}, \widetilde{c}_{os}, \widetilde{z}_{os})$  for message  $\mu$ , check that  $\phi_{os}(\widetilde{z}_{os}) = \widetilde{A}_{os} + \widetilde{c}_{os} \cdot W$  and  $\widetilde{c}_{os} = H(W, \widetilde{A}_{os}, \mu)$ .

Note that  $\sigma$  verifies by linearity of  $\phi_{os}$ . The scheme is proven one-more unforgeable for polylog-many signing sessions [PS00, HKL19]. This bound is tight due to an efficient attack on the ROS problem by Benhamouda et al. [BLL+21, BLL+22] which undermines the concurrent security of BS<sub>os</sub>. We aim to restore security of BS<sub>os</sub> with additional measures. For exposition, it will be instructive to give intuition for the security argument and why it fails to generalize to many concurrent sessions. We sketch the argument for one-more unforgeability (OMUF) and discuss the extension to one-more *strong* unforgeability (OMSUF) later.

OMUF ANALYSIS FOR OKAMOTO-SCHNORR. Recall that in the OMUF game, the adversary obtains the public key W, and access to signing oracles  $S_1$  and  $S_2$  which model the 1-st and 2-nd signer message in the signing protocol, respectively. In particular, the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  can obtain valid transcripts  $\tau_{os} = (A_{os}, c_{os}, z_{os})$ for the public key W via oracles  $S_1$  and  $S_2$ , where  $c_{os}$  is under A's control. The adversary's goal is to forge  $\ell+1$  signature-message pairs  $(\sigma_1,\mu_1),\ldots,(\sigma_{\ell+1},\mu_{\ell+1})$  for distinct messages, while completing at most  $\ell$ signing sessions. The idea behind  $BS_{os}$  is that the underlying  $\Sigma$ -protocol is witness-indistinguishable, that is, the transcripts  $\tau_{os}$  do not reveal any information about the secret key w. Indeed, this property is leveraged to show that the scheme is secure for at most polylog-many signing sessions [PS00, HKL19]. Roughly, the reduction embeds a DL challenge H into the parameters and rewinds the adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  on a hash query  $c_i = \mathsf{H}(W, A_i, \mu_i)$  associated to one of  $\mathcal{A}$ 's signatures  $\sigma_i = (A_i, c_i, z_i)$  for message  $\mu_i$ . From the second run, the adversary obtains a signature  $\sigma'_i = (A_i, c'_i, z'_i)$  with fresh challenge  $c'_i \neq c_i$ . In particular, the signatures  $\sigma_i$ and  $\sigma'_i$  form related  $\Sigma$ -protocol transcripts, therefore the reduction can recover some witness  $\boldsymbol{w}' = (w'_0, w'_1)$ such that  $\phi_{os}(w') = W$  by 2-special soundness of the  $\Sigma$ -protocol. If at most polylog-many signing sessions are completed, then [PS00, HKL19] show that  $\mathbf{w}' \neq \mathbf{w}$  with sufficient probability. If so, we have  $\phi_{os}(\mathbf{w}' - \mathbf{w}) = 0_{\mathbb{G}}$ with  $\mathbf{w}' - \mathbf{w} \neq 0$  by linearity of  $\phi_{os}$ . In that case, the reduction can compute the discrete logarithm of H, breaking DL.

Note that it is tempting to argue that "as the adversary learns nothing about the witness  $\boldsymbol{w}$  from the initial run, the extracted witness  $\boldsymbol{w}'$  must be fresh in general." However, this argument is flawed as  $\boldsymbol{w}'$  may depend on the distribution of the honest transcripts  $\tau_{os}$  during the initial and second run, which are issued during signing via the secret key  $\boldsymbol{w}$ . Further, the transcripts from initial and second runs together reveal  $\boldsymbol{w}$  in some scenarios with concurrent sessions. For example, if a session with nonce  $A_{os}$  is opened but not closed before the adversary is rewound, then the reduction may provide two valid transcripts for nonce  $A_{os}$ . These related transcripts lead to an extraction of the original secret key  $\boldsymbol{w}$ . If all of  $\mathcal{A}$ 's signatures are somehow built from the issued transcripts, then there is no way to argue that the extracted witness  $\boldsymbol{w}'$  is different from  $\boldsymbol{w}$ .

We note that prior attempts [AO00, Abe01, AHJ21] to formalize the above intuition resulted in errors. At this point, there is no known proof technique to argue OMUF via witness-indistinguishability and rewinding beyond polylog-many concurrent sessions. Indeed, there is no hope to formally show such a result for BS<sub>os</sub> due to the attack on ROS [BLL<sup>+</sup>21, BLL<sup>+</sup>22].

<u>Our ideal</u>. We resolve the apparent issue sketched above by enforcing that the extracted witness is determined in the initial run *statistically* from the adversary's signatures. That is, we base our blind signature on  $\mathsf{BS}_{\mathsf{os}}$ , but we introduce additional measures such that  $\mathcal{A}$ 's signatures  $(\sigma_1, \mu_1), \ldots, (\sigma_{\ell+1}, \mu_{\ell+1})$  determine the extracted witness  $\boldsymbol{w}'$  information-theoretically *before* any rewinding occurs. As the initial run hides the secret key  $\boldsymbol{w}$  perfectly, the witness  $\boldsymbol{w}'$  extracted via rewinding later on must be distributed independently

{5}------------------------------------------------

from w; in particular w<sup>1</sup> ‰ w with sufficient probability as desired. Both w and w<sup>1</sup> together allow us to compute the discrete logarithm of H as in the original analysis in [\[PS00\]](#page-53-6), except there is no bound on the number of concurrent sessions.

Our design. Let us ignore blindness at this step for exposition. In short, we tag the Okamoto-Schnorr nonce Aos with an algebraic MAC σmac and let the signer issue a Σ-protocol transcript τmac " pAmac, cmac, zmacq that certifies that σmac is indeed a valid tag for Aos. The transcript τmac is compiled into a proof via Fiat-Shamir by the user. The signature then consists of σ " pτos, τmac, σmacq. Let us inspect the adversary's signatures in the reduction under this modification:

Case 1 (Nonce collision). If there are two distinct signatures with identical nonces Aos,i and Aos,j amongst A's output, then the reduction sets w<sup>1</sup> " pzos,i ´ zos,j q { pcos,i ´ cos,j q and computes the discrete logarithm of H via w ´ w<sup>1</sup> . Note that w is the secret key that the reduction samples to simulate the game, chosen such that ϕospwq " W. To see why this works, observe that by linearity of ϕos, the verification equations give

<span id="page-5-0"></span>
$$\phi_{os}(z_{os,i}) - c_{os,i} \cdot W = \phi_{os}(z_{os,j}) - c_{os,j} \cdot W \qquad \Longrightarrow \qquad \phi_{os}(\mathbf{w}') = W. \tag{1}$$

Note that cos,i ‰ cos,j except for negligible probability as µ<sup>i</sup> ‰ µ<sup>j</sup> . Also, it must hold that w ‰ w<sup>1</sup> with sufficient probability as the adversary's view V<sup>A</sup> is independent of the secret key w chosen by the reduction, however, the extracted w<sup>1</sup> is determined entirely by VA. We formalize this argument with the technique introduced by [\[PS00\]](#page-53-6): we exhibit a map Ψ which preserves the adversary's view, and therefore the witness w<sup>1</sup> determined by VA, yet the witness w used for simulation is mapped to a different value. In summary, we know that ϕospwq " W " ϕospw<sup>1</sup> q (as the view is preserved) with w ‰ w<sup>1</sup> . Therefore, we can compute the DLOG of H as before.

Case 2 (Distinct nonces). If all ℓ`1 forgeries σ<sup>i</sup> have distinct nonces Aos,i, we can produce a MAC forgery. That is, observe that the proof τmac,i from σ<sup>i</sup> ensures that the tags σmac,i from the forgeries are valid. As only ℓ-many tags were issued and the Σ-protocol proof ensures that these tags are valid, this breaks unforgeability of the underlying MAC. We note that to simulate the transcripts τmac during signing, we require an additional simulation trapdoor for the Σ-protocol. Looking ahead, we will implement with an OR-proof.

Note that at this point, no rewinding is required to extract the ϕos-preimage w<sup>1</sup> which simplifies the analysis. However, the protocol is not yet blind and looking ahead, rewinding will become necessary after we ensure blindness with additional measures.

Blinding the signatures. We must ensure that an issued signature σ " pτos, τmac, σmacq derived by the user is hard to be linked to its signing session. The first step is that the user blinds the proofs τmac and τos into τrmac and τros, respectively. Unfortunately, the tag σmac remains linkable to the signing session. Even if we somehow randomize σmac, the tag still verifies for the nonce Aos from the signing session, leading to trivial attacks on blindness. Furthermore, if we blind <sup>A</sup>os into <sup>A</sup>ros, the tag <sup>σ</sup>mac tags the original nonce <sup>A</sup>os, not <sup>A</sup>ros from the signature as desired. Our solution is as follows.

Blind the MAC. We blind σmac to σrmac with blinding factor rrnd such that σrmac remains a valid tag for the original nonce Aos but cannot be linked back to σmac. Furthermore, given rrnd, it is possible to recompute σmac.

Commit to blinding factors. The user commits to the tag blinding factor rrnd and the original nonce Aos, together with the nonce's blinding factors α and γ, in a commitment cmtrnd.

Prove honest blinding. The user proves that the blinded nonce <sup>A</sup>ros and tag <sup>σ</sup>rmac satisfy the following properties with respect to the values committed in cmtrnd. Note that we assume that cmtrnd is perfectly binding to ensure that these relations hold without rewinding (cf. discussion below):

- 1. the blinded tag σrmac is obtained by blinding σmac via rrnd;
- 2. the blinded nonce <sup>A</sup>ros is obtained by blinding <sup>A</sup>os via <sup>p</sup>α, γq;
- 3. the original tag σmac is valid for the original nonce Aos.

{6}------------------------------------------------

The first two points are proven with a Fiat-Shamir compiled proof  $\tau_{\mathsf{rnd}} = (\widetilde{A}_{\mathsf{rnd}}, \widetilde{c}_{\mathsf{rnd}}, \widetilde{z}_{\mathsf{rnd}})$  computed by the user. Note that the user cannot prove the final point as it cannot verify  $\sigma_{\mathsf{mac}}$  itself. To ensure the validity of the original tag  $\sigma_{\mathsf{mac}}$ , the user adapts the proof  $\tau_{\mathsf{mac}}$  accordingly, leveraging linear properties of the transcript  $\tau_{\mathsf{mac}}$  and the fact that the blinding  $\sigma_{\mathsf{mac}}$  into  $\widetilde{\sigma}_{\mathsf{mac}}$  is linear, similar to [CATZ24, OTZZ24, KRW24, KR25].

As alluded to above, we further require a trapdoor for the  $\tau_{\mathsf{mac}}$  proof for simulation, which we implement by a DDH-trapdoor. That is, the public parameters contain a tuple D. The proof  $\tau_{\mathsf{mac}}$  is implemented as OR-proof: either it ensures that the MAC is valid or that D is a DDH-tuple, similar to [KRW24]. This gives us the freedom to issue simulated tags in the security analysis, while being able to simulate well-distributed proofs.

We further ensure that  $\operatorname{cmt}_{\mathsf{rnd}}$  is extractable (i.e., there is a trapdoor with which the  $\mathit{unique}$  opening for  $\operatorname{cmt}_{\mathsf{rnd}}$  can be recovered). This is important to argue about the distribution of the committed values in  $\operatorname{cmt}_{\mathsf{rnd}}$  in the initial run (i.e., before rewinding). As consequence, we can only commit to group elements directly, therefore we commit to the blinding factors in  $\mathbb{Z}_p$  in the exponent.

PUTTING EVERYTHING TOGETHER. In summary, the public parameters contain the generators (G, H) for Okamoto-Schnorr, a tuple D and a commitment key bck for a commitment  $C_{rnd}$ . The public key is  $W = \phi_{os}(w)$  for  $w \leftarrow_{\mathbb{Z}_p^2}$ , whereas the secret key is w and the signing key msk for the MAC. The signer-user interaction issues a  $\Sigma$ -protocol transcript  $\tau_{os} = (A_{os}, c_{os}, z_{os})$  for knowledge of w to the user, and further a tag  $\sigma_{mac}$  on  $A_{os}$  and an OR-proof  $\tau_{or} = (A_{or}, c_{or}, z_{or})$  that either  $\sigma_{mac}$  is valid or D is a DDH-tuple. The user blinds the transcripts  $\tau_{os}$  into  $\tilde{\tau}_{os}$ , and further blinds  $\tau_{or}$  together with the tag  $\sigma_{mac}$  into  $\tilde{\tau}_{or}$  and  $\tilde{\sigma}_{mac}$ , respectively, as described above. At the same time, the user compiles the transcripts  $\tilde{\tau}_{os}$  and  $\tilde{\tau}_{or}$  via Fiat-Shamir. Finally, the user commits to the blinding factors in cmt<sub>rnd</sub> and computes a Fiat-Shamir proof  $\tilde{\tau}_{rnd}$  for honest blinding as described above. The derived signature is  $\sigma \leftarrow (\tilde{\sigma}_{mac}, \text{cmt}_{rnd}, \tilde{\tau}_{os}, \tilde{\tau}_{or}, \tilde{\tau}_{rnd})$  For a detailed description of our construction and its building blocks, we refer to Section 3.

Blindness follows from standard arguments. Notably, if cmt<sub>rnd</sub> is statistically hiding, then blindness also holds statistically. We outline the OMUF proof below.

PROVING ONE-MORE UNFORGEABILITY. OMUF still relies on the high-level intuition sketched above, however, the proof becomes much more technical due to the additional blinding factors. In particular, we assume that cmt<sub>rnd</sub> is setup such that the game can extract the messages. We stress that  $A_{os}$  can be extracted in clear, however, the blinding factors can only be recovered in the exponent. In the overview below, we assume that  $A_{os,i}$  has been extracted from all of  $\mathcal{A}$ 's signature outputs  $\sigma_i$  for  $i \in [\ell + 1]$ . Let us consider the two cases outlined above in more detail.

Case 1 (Nonce collision). If there are two distinct signatures with identical extracted nonces  $A_{os,i} = A_{os,j}$  amongst  $\mathcal{A}$ 's outputs, then the reduction rewinds the adversary to extract some  $\phi_{os}$ -preimage  $\boldsymbol{w}'$  for W from the transcripts. Then, the reduction computes the DLOG of H via  $\boldsymbol{w}$  and  $\boldsymbol{w}'$ , where  $\boldsymbol{w}$  is the secret key chosen by the reduction to simulate the game.

That is, observe that the Okamoto-Schnorr blinding factors  $(\alpha_i, \gamma_i)$  and  $(\alpha_j, \gamma_j)$  are determined by  $\operatorname{cmt}_{\mathsf{rnd},i}$  and  $\operatorname{cmt}_{\mathsf{rnd},j}$  in the initial run, respectively, in the exponent. Also,  $A_{\mathsf{os},i} = A_{\mathsf{os},j}$  is known before any rewinding occurs. Therefore, rewinding is guaranteed to recover exactly these blinding factors. Given the blinding factors and  $A_{\mathsf{os},i}$ , the reduction computes the desired preimage  $\boldsymbol{w}'$  similar to Equation (1), taking into account the additional blinding factors. We stress again that the extracted witness is already determined through the first run, even though there may be a correlation between the initial and rewound transcripts. This allows us to argue that  $\boldsymbol{w}' \neq \boldsymbol{w}$ , and as a result, the reduction can compute the DLOG of H as before.

Case 2 (Distinct nonces). If all extracted Okamoto-Schnorr nonces are distinct, then there must be a fresh nonce  $A_{os,i}$  that the game did not tag via the MAC scheme. Note that the MAC  $\tilde{\sigma}_{mac,i}$  is blinded and we must first recover  $r_{rnd,i}$  to recompute the tag  $\sigma_{mac}$  on  $A_{os,i}$ . This is possible via rewinding which allows us to reduce to unforgeability of the MAC scheme. There is an important subtlety in the proof: the reduction may tag the nonce  $A_{os,i}$  during rewinding. In this case, we cannot argue that  $\sigma_{mac,i}$  constitutes a forgery. However, notice that the reduction exclusively tags the nonces  $A_{os}$  during the simulation of

{7}------------------------------------------------

 $S_1$ . In particular, these nonces are in control of the reduction and can be sampled *before* any rewinding occurs. This allows to argue that  $A_{os,i}$  is tagged neither in the initial run, nor during rewinding.

An interesting observation is that since the reduction only issues MAC tags on random nonces, it suffices if the MAC is secure if the adversary sees tags on random messages.

<u>INSTANTIATIONS.</u> To instantiate the above template, we require an appropriate MAC and commitment. For the latter, we either choose a multi-recipient variant of ElGamal (for computational blindness and compact signatures) or Groth-Sahai commitments (for statistical blindness but larger signatures). For the former, we note that the MAC must be algebraic and blinding-friendly (i.e., there is a  $\Sigma$ -protocol for the validity of tags that can be blinded together with the tag). We employ the DDH-based MAC  $M_{alg}$  by Chase et al. [CMZ14]. Note that  $M_{alg}$  only allows tagging  $\mathbb{Z}_p$  elements, but this is sufficient for our purpose due to the following insight. A valid tag on message  $m \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  has the form

$$U = uG$$
,  $V = zU$ ,  $S = u(x_0G + x_1mG)$ ,  $T = u(y_0G + y_1mG)$ ,

where  $(z, x_0, x_1, y_0, y_1)$  are part of the secret key and  $u \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^*$  is the randomness. Instead, we simply compute tags on group elements  $M \in \mathbb{G}$  via

$$U = uG$$
,  $V = zU$ ,  $S = u(x_0G + x_1 \bar{M})$ ,  $T = u(y_0G + y_1 \bar{M})$ .

If there is a collision in the nonces  $A_{os,i}$ , then we do not require the security of the MAC, in particular, the structure of  $\mathsf{M}_{\mathsf{alg}}$  is irrelevant. Otherwise, the reduction can know the discrete logarithm h of H. In particular, the nonces  $A_{\mathsf{os}}$  can be expressed as  $A_{\mathsf{os}} = r_0 G + r_1 H = (r_0 + h r_1)G$ , so the reduction can instead issue a tag for message  $m = r_0 + h r_1$ . Furthermore, the fresh nonce  $A_{\mathsf{os},i}$  extracted from  $\mathcal{A}$ 's outputs in the reduction can be expressed with known discrete logarithm through knowledge of the blinding factors and the verification equation of  $\Sigma_{\mathsf{os}}$ . This allows us to reduce to MAC security with  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ -messages.

EXTENSIONS. While we sketched the security proof for OMUF, it is possible to prove that the scheme satisfies one-more strong unforgeability (OMSUF). This mainly relies on the (computational) unique response property of the  $\Sigma$ -protocols. Furthermore, we can prove partial blindness if the DDH-trapdoor  $\boldsymbol{D}$  is sampled with a random oracle  $H_{pb}$  evaluated on the common message info. This adds additional complexity in the OMSUF proof, since we must issue additional tags on Okamoto-Schnorr nonces in sessions for distinct common messages. We refer to Section 3.2 for more details.

#### <span id="page-7-0"></span>1.2 Related and Future Work

This paper shows that it is possible to construct three-move blind signatures in pairing-free groups in the ROM alone, resolving a major open problem in the field. We still rely on the DDH assumption for our security analysis; the "dream result" in the field is a three-move blind signature from DL. The recent work [KLR25] gives a complementary result to ours by constructing a four-move blind signature from DL. It remains unclear how to reconcile our techniques and the techniques from [KR25] into a three-move blind signature from DL. We briefly discuss the challenges and leave the problem for future work.

- Our construction relies on an algebraic MAC and a linearly-homomorphic extractable commitment. Both primitives are well-known to exist from DDH. It is unclear how to construct these from DL alone in a black-box manner.
- In the scheme by Klooß et al. [KLR25], signatures consist of a Fiat-Shamir proof for a message-dependent statement. The statement can be punctured for some message  $m^*$ . That is, the reduction can produce a proof (and therefore simulate signing) for any statement associated to some  $m \neq m^*$ , while a proof for statement  $m^*$  allows to solve DL with a rewinding-based argument. However, as the statement for the to-be-issued proof depends on the message, the user must embed the message into the signing session in the first move. The three additional moves then establish the Fiat-Shamir proof. It is unclear how to employ our techniques to compress their scheme down to three moves.

{8}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-8-0"></span>2 Preliminaries

NOTATION. Let  $[n..m] = \{n, n+1, ..., m\}$  for any  $n, m \in \mathbb{Z}$  where  $n \leq m$  and [n] = [1..n] for any  $n \geq 1$ . We use  $\lambda$  as the security parameter.

<u>Pairing-free Groups.</u> Let **GGen** be an algorithm that on input  $1^{\lambda}$  outputs a group description  $\mathbb{G}$  of  $\lambda$ -bit prime-order  $p = p(\lambda)$  with generator G. We employ additive notation. Throughout, we assume that algorithms obtain as input  $(\mathbb{G}, p, G) \leftarrow \mathbb{G}(1^{\lambda})$  as parameter implicitly. Also, we denote by  $\operatorname{dlog}_G X$  the discrete logarithm  $x \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  of X with basis G, where xG = X. We extend the notation to vectors of group elements  $(X_1, \ldots, X_n)$  in the natural manner.

SECURITY ASSUMPTIONS. We recall the DL and DDH assumptions. Let  $(\mathbb{G}, p, G)$  as above. We note that the assumptions depend on GGen employed to (implicitly) sample  $(\mathbb{G}, p, G)$ .

**Definition 2.1** (DL **Assumption).** The discrete logarithm (DL) assumption holds if for any PPT adversary A, it holds that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{dl}}_{\mathsf{GGen}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) := \mathsf{Pr}[x = x' \mid x \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p, x' \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(G,xG)] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \; .$$

**Definition 2.2** (DDH **Assumption).** The decisional Diffie-Hellman (DDH) assumption holds if for any PPT adversary A, it holds that

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ddh}}_{\mathsf{GGen}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) := \Big| \mathsf{Pr}[a,b &\leftarrow \mathbb{s} \, \mathbb{Z}_p : \mathcal{A}(G,aG,bG,(ab)G) = 1] - \\ & \mathsf{Pr}[a,b,c &\leftarrow \mathbb{s} \, \mathbb{Z}_p : \mathcal{A}(G,aG,bG,cG) = 1] \Big| = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \; . \end{split}$$

**Definition 2.3** (QDDH **Assumption).** The Q-fold (for  $Q = Q(\lambda)$ ) decisional Diffie-Hellman assumption holds if for any PPT adversary A, it holds that the following advantage is negligible:

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{qddh}}_{\mathsf{GGen},Q}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) := \left| \mathsf{Pr}[a \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p, \boldsymbol{b} \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^Q : \mathcal{A}(G,aG,(b_iG,(ab_i)G)_{i \in [Q]}) = 1] - \right|$$
$$\mathsf{Pr}[a \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p, \boldsymbol{b}, \boldsymbol{c} \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^Q : \mathcal{A}(G,aG,(b_iG,c_iG)_{i \in [Q]}) = 1] \right|.$$

The DDH assumption tightly implies the QDDH assumption. Concretely, for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  with roughly the same running time such that  $\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{qddh}}_{\mathsf{GGen},Q}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) \leqslant \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ddh}}_{\mathsf{GGen}}(\mathcal{B},\lambda) + \frac{1}{p-1}$  [EHK+13].

<u>BLIND SIGNATURES.</u> Now, we define blind signatures [Cha82]. We consider 3-move schemes with partial blindness [AF96]. A partially blind signature BS is a tuple of PPT algorithms (BS.Setup, BS.KeyGen, (BS.Sign<sub>1</sub>, BS.U<sub>1</sub>, BS.Sign<sub>2</sub>, BS.U<sub>2</sub>), BS.Ver) such that

- $\mathsf{BS}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda) \to \mathsf{pp}$ : Given security parameter  $1^\lambda$ , outputs public parameters  $\mathsf{pp}$ .
- BS.KeyGen(pp)  $\rightarrow$  (sk, pk): Given public parameters pp, outputs a secret key sk and public key pk.
- $BS.Sign_1(pp, sk, info) \rightarrow (smsg_1, st_S)$ : Given public parameters pp, a secret key sk and a common message info, outputs a signer message  $smsg_1$  and signer  $state st_S$ .
- $\mathsf{BS.U}_1(\mathsf{pp},\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{info},\mu,\mathsf{smsg}_1) \to (\mathsf{umsg},\mathsf{st}_\mathsf{U})$ : Given public parameters  $\mathsf{pp}$ , a public key  $\mathsf{pk}$ , a common message  $\mathsf{info}$ , a message  $\mu$  and a signer message outputs  $\mathsf{smsg}_1$ , outputs a user message  $\mathsf{umsg}$  and user state  $\mathsf{st}_\mathsf{U}$ .
- $BS.Sign_2(umsg, st_S) \rightarrow smsg_2$ : Given public parameters pp, a secret key sk, a user message umsg and signer state  $st_S$ , outputs a signer message  $smsg_2$ .
- $BS.U_2(smsg_2, st_U) \rightarrow \sigma$ : Given signer message  $smsg_2$  and user state  $st_U$ , outputs a signature  $\sigma$ .
- BS.Ver(pp, pk, info,  $\mu$ ,  $\sigma$ )  $\rightarrow$  b: Given public parameters pp, a public key pk, a common message info, a message  $\mu$  and a signature  $\sigma$ , deterministically outputs  $b \in \{0, 1\}$ .

If clear by context, we leave the user and signer states implicit for conciseness. We define correctness, partial blindness and one-more strong unforgeability below.

| $4:  (info^{\textstyle *}, (\mu^{(i)}, \sigma^{(i)})_{i \in [\ell]}) \longleftarrow \mathcal{A}^{\mathrm{S}_1, \mathrm{S}_2}(pp, vk)$       | Oracle | $\mathrm{e}\;\mathrm{S}_{2}(\mathrm{sid},umsg)$                                        |

**Fig. 1.** One-More (Strong) Unforgeability security game of BS. The dashed box indicates the winning condition of the OMUF game, while the solid box indicates the winning condition of the OMSUF game.

**Definition 2.4 (Correctness).** A partially blind signature BS is (perfectly) correct if for all public parameters  $pp \in BS.Setup(1^{\lambda})$ ,  $(sk, pk) \in BS.KeyGen(pp)$ , all messages  $\mu$  and all common messages info, it holds that  $BS.Ver(pp, pk, info, \mu, \sigma) = 1$ , where

<span id="page-9-0"></span>
$$\begin{aligned} (\mathsf{smsg}_1, \mathsf{st}_\mathsf{S}) &\in \mathsf{BS}.\mathsf{Sign}_1(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{sk}, \mathsf{info}) \\ (\mathsf{umsg}, \mathsf{st}_\mathsf{U}) &\in \mathsf{BS}.\mathsf{U}_1(\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{info}, \mu, \mathsf{smsg}_1) \\ \mathsf{smsg}_2 &\in \mathsf{BS}.\mathsf{Sign}_2(\mathsf{umsg}, \mathsf{st}_\mathsf{S}) \\ \sigma &\in \mathsf{BS}.\mathsf{U}_2(\mathsf{smsg}_2, \mathsf{st}_\mathsf{U}) \end{aligned}$$

One-more strong unforgeability ensures that it is hard to compute a fresh signature-message pair without interacting with the signer.

**Definition 2.5 (One-More (Strong) Unforgeability).** A partially blind signature scheme BS is one-more (strong) unforgeable if for any  $PPT \ A$  in the game OMUF (or OMSUF) as defined in Figure 1, its advantage defined as follows is negligible.

<span id="page-9-1"></span>
$$\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{BS}}^{\mathrm{omuf/omsuf}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) := \mathsf{Pr}[(\mathrm{OMUF/OMSUF})_{\mathsf{BS}}^{\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) = 1] \ .$$

The following lemma also allows us to assume that any OM(S)UF adversary finishes all signing sessions. Note that similar assumption has been (implicitly) made in prior works on three-move blind signatures [FPS20, TZ22]. We give a proof of the lemma in Appendix A. Moreover, the lemma extends naturally for adversaries in the ROM (with increased random oracle query count).

**Lemma 2.6.** Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be an adversary against OMUF (resp. OMSUF) game of BS, running in time  $T_{\mathcal{A}} = T_{\mathcal{A}}(\lambda)$  and making  $Q_{S_1} = Q_{S_1}(\lambda), Q_{S_2} = Q_{S_2}(\lambda)$  to  $S_1, S_2$ , respectively. Then, there exists an adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  against OMUF (resp. OMSUF) game of BS, running in time  $T_{\mathcal{B}} \approx T_{\mathcal{A}}$  and making  $Q_S = Q_{S_1}$  queries to both  $S_1, S_2$ , such that

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathrm{omuf}}_{\mathsf{BS}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) \leqslant \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathrm{omuf}}_{\mathsf{BS}}(\mathcal{B},\lambda) \;, \qquad \qquad \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathrm{omsuf}}_{\mathsf{BS}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) \leqslant \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathrm{omsuf}}_{\mathsf{BS}}(\mathcal{B},\lambda) \;.$$

Partial blindness ensures that the signer cannot link signing interactions to the issued signatures, even if the signing key is malicious.

**Definition 2.7 (Partial Blindness).** We say that BS is partially blind if for any adversary A in the game Blind as defined in Figure 2, its advantage defined as follows is negligible.

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{blind}}_{\mathsf{BS}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) := \left| \mathsf{Pr}[\mathsf{Blind}^{\mathcal{A}}_{\mathsf{BS}}(\lambda) = 1] - \frac{1}{2} \right| \; .$$

<span id="page-10-0"></span>Fig. 2. Blindness security game of BS.

COMMITMENT. A commitment scheme C with message space  $C.\mathcal{M}$ , key space  $C.\mathcal{K}$ , commitment space  $C.\mathcal{C}$  and randomness space  $C.\mathcal{R}$  is a tuple of PPT algorithms (C.Setup, C.Commit) such that

 $\mathsf{C.Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \to \mathsf{ck}$ : Given security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$ , outputs commitment key  $\mathsf{ck}$ .

C.Commit(ck, m; r)  $\rightarrow$  cmt: Given the commitment key ck, message  $m \in C.\mathcal{M}$  and explicit randomness  $r \in C.\mathcal{R}$ , it outputs a commitment c to m.

We do not define an explicit verification algorithms, instead we verify the whether a commitment cmt opens to message m with randomness r via cmt = C.Commit(ck, m; r). We say that C is linearly-homomorphic if  $C.\mathcal{M}$ ,  $C.\mathcal{C}$  and  $C.\mathcal{R}$  are  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ -vector spaces and if for each commitment key  $\mathsf{ck} \in C.\mathcal{K}$ , the commitment function  $C.C(\mathsf{ck}, \cdot; \cdot) : (m, r) \mapsto \mathsf{cmt}$  is  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ -linear. Below, we recall desirable security properties of commitment schemes.

**Definition 2.8 (Hiding).** A commitment scheme C is computationally hiding if for any PPT adversary A, we have that the following advantage is negligible

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{hide}}_{\mathsf{C}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) = \left| \mathsf{Pr} \left[ \ b = b' \left| \begin{matrix} \mathsf{ck} \leftarrow \! \! \$ \, \mathsf{C.Setup}(1^{\lambda}), & (m_0,m_1,\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{A}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{pp}), \\ b \leftarrow \! \! \$ \, \{0,1\}, & r \leftarrow \! \! \$ \, \mathsf{C.}\mathcal{R}, & b' \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(c_b,\mathsf{st}_{\mathcal{A}}) \end{matrix} \right. \right| - \frac{1}{2} \right| \ ,$$

where  $c \leftarrow \mathsf{C.Commit}(\mathsf{ck}, m_b; r)$ . We say that  $\mathsf{C}$  is statistically hiding if the above holds for all **potentially** inefficient adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ .

**Definition 2.9 ((Strong) Binding).** A commitment scheme C is computationally binding if for any PPT adversary A, we have

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{bind}}_{\mathsf{C}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) := \mathsf{Pr}\left[\left.m_0 \neq m_1 \wedge c_0 = c_1 \left| \begin{matrix} \mathsf{ck} \leftarrow \mathsf{C.Setup}(1^\lambda), \\ (m_0,m_1,r_0,r_1) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{ck}) \end{matrix}\right.\right] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda),$$

where  $c_i = \mathsf{C.Commit}(\mathsf{ck}, m_i; r_i)$  for  $i \in \{0, 1\}$ . We say that  $\mathsf{C}$  is computationally "strongly" binding if the winning condition above is  $(m_0 \neq m_1 \vee r_0 \neq r_1) \wedge c_0 = c_1$ , and we denote the advantage with  $\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{sbind}}_\mathsf{C}(\mathcal{A}, \lambda)$  instead. We say the scheme is perfectly (strongly) binding if the advantage term above is 0 for all potentially inefficient adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ .

**Definition 2.10 (Extractability).** A commitment scheme C is extractable if there exists PPT algorithms Ext = (C.ExtSetup, C.Extract) defined as follows

C.ExtSetup( $1^{\lambda}$ )  $\rightarrow$  (ck, td) : Generates a commitment key ck and a trapdoor td, and outputs (ck, td).

 $\mathsf{C.Extract}(\mathsf{td},c) \to m : On \ input \ of \ a \ trapdoor \ \mathsf{td} \ and \ a \ committee \ to \ \mathsf{C.C.}, \ deterministically \ outputs \ the \ committee \ message \ m \in \mathsf{C.}M.$

| Game UF-RMA $_{M}^{\mathcal{A}}(\lambda)$ :                                                | Oracle Tag():                                       |
|--------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|-----------------------------------------------------|
| 1: $Q \leftarrow \emptyset$                                                                | 1 : <i>m</i> ←\$ M. <i>M</i>                        |
| 2: $mpp \leftarrow M.Setup(1^{\lambda}); (msk, ipp) \leftarrow M.KeyGen(mpp)$              | $2:  \sigma \leftarrow \$ \; M.Tag(mpp,msk,m)$      |
| $3: (m^*, \sigma^*) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}^{\mathrm{Tag}}(mpp, ipp)$                       | $3:  \mathcal{Q} \leftarrow \mathcal{Q} \cup \{m\}$ |
| 4: <b>return</b> $m^* \notin \mathcal{Q} \wedge M.Ver(mpp,ipp,msk,m^*,\sigma^*_{alg}) = 1$ | $4:  \mathbf{return} (m,\sigma)$                    |

Fig. 3. UF-RMA security game of M.

We require that:

(a) For any PPT adversary A, the random variables  $\mathsf{ck}_{\mathsf{real}}$  and  $\mathsf{ck}_{\mathsf{sim}}$  are indistinguishable, and we denote the advantage as

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ext-dist}}_{\mathsf{C},\mathsf{Ext}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) := |\mathsf{Pr}[\mathcal{A}(\mathsf{ck}_{\mathsf{real}}) = 1] - \mathsf{Pr}[\mathcal{A}(\mathsf{ck}_{\mathsf{sim}}) = 1]| \;,$$

where  $\mathsf{ck}_{\mathsf{real}} \leftarrow \mathsf{s} \mathsf{C.Setup}(1^{\lambda})$  and  $(\mathsf{ck}_{\mathsf{sim}}, \mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{s} \mathsf{C.ExtSetup}(1^{\lambda})$ .

(b) For all  $(\mathsf{ck}, \mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{sC.ExtSetup}(1^{\lambda}), m \in \mathsf{C}.\mathcal{M}, r \in \mathsf{C}.\mathcal{R} \ and \ c \leftarrow \mathsf{C.Commit}(\mathsf{ck}, m; r) \in \mathsf{C}.\mathcal{C}, \ it \ holds \ that m = \mathsf{C.Extract}(\mathsf{ck}, \mathsf{td}, c).$ 

MESSAGE AUTHENTICATION CODES. We recall syntax of a Message Authentication Code (MAC) and recall the unforgeability against random message attack security (UF-RMA). A MAC M consists of (probabilistic) algorithms (M.Setup, M.KeyGen, M.Tag, M.Ver) which have the following syntax:

M.Setup( $1^{\lambda}$ )  $\rightarrow$  mpp: Generates the public parameters mpp which implicitly defines the message space M. $\mathcal{M}$ . M.KeyGen(mpp)  $\rightarrow$  (msk, ipp): Generates the issuer's secret key msk and its corresponding issuer's public parameters ipp.

 $\mathsf{M}.\mathsf{Tag}(\mathsf{mpp},\mathsf{msk},m\in\mathsf{M}.\mathcal{M})\to\sigma$ : For a message  $m\in\mathbb{Z}_p$ , outputs the tag  $\sigma$ .

M.Ver(mpp, ipp, msk,  $m, \sigma$ )  $\rightarrow$  {0, 1}: Verify whether the message-tag pair  $(m, \sigma_{alg})$  is valid with respect to msk.

**Definition 2.11 (UF-RMA of M).** A MAC M is unforgeable against random message attack (UF-RMA) if for any PPT adversary A, the following advantage is negligible.

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ufrma}}_{\mathsf{M}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) := \mathsf{Pr}[\mathrm{UF}\text{-}\mathrm{RMA}^{\mathcal{A}}_{\mathsf{M}}(\lambda) = 1]$$
.

 $\underline{\Sigma\text{-PROTOCOLS.}}$  We establish  $\Sigma$ -protocol notation and security. Let  $\mathfrak{R} = \{(\mathfrak{x}, \mathfrak{w})\}$  be an NP-relation and denote by  $\mathfrak{L}_{\mathfrak{R}} = \{\mathfrak{x} \mid \exists \mathfrak{w} \text{ s.t. } (\mathfrak{x}, \mathfrak{w}) \in \mathfrak{R}\}$  the induced language. A parameterized  $\Sigma$ -protocol  $\Sigma$  for  $\mathfrak{R}$  with challenge space  $\Sigma.\mathcal{C}$  is a tuple of PPT algorithms ( $\Sigma$ .Init,  $\Sigma$ .Resp,  $\Sigma$ .Ver), where  $\Sigma$ .Resp and  $\Sigma$ .Ver are deterministic, such that

 $\Sigma.\mathsf{Init}(\mathbb{x},\mathbb{w}) \to A$ : Given a statement-witness pair  $(\mathbb{x},\mathbb{w}) \in \mathfrak{R}$ , outputs a first flow message (i.e., nonce) A and a state st, where we assume st includes  $(\mathbb{x},\mathbb{w})$ .

 $\Sigma$ .Resp(st, c)  $\to z$ : Given a state st and a challenge  $c \in \Sigma$ . $\mathcal{C}$ , outputs a third flow message (i.e., response) z.  $\Sigma$ .Ver(x, A, c, z)  $\to b$ : Given a statement x, a nonce A, challenge  $c \in \Sigma$ . $\mathcal{C}$ , and a response z, outputs a bit  $b \in \{0,1\}$ .

We refer to the tuple  $\tau = (A, c, z)$  as transcript. We say that  $\tau$  is valid for x if Ver(x, A, c, z) outputs 1. We may omit x if clear by context. We recall desired security notions for  $\Sigma$ -protocols below, following [KR25].

**Definition 2.12 (Correctness).** Let  $\mathfrak{R}$  be an NP-relation and  $\Sigma$  be a  $\Sigma$ -protocol for  $\mathfrak{R}$  with challenge space  $\Sigma.\mathcal{C}$ . We say  $\Sigma$  is correct, if for all  $(x, w) \in \mathfrak{R}$ ,

$$\Pr[\mathsf{Ver}(\mathbf{x},A,c,z)=1\mid (A,\mathsf{st}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}.\mathsf{Init}(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{w});\ c \leftarrow \mathcal{S}.\mathcal{C};\ z \leftarrow \mathcal{S}.\mathsf{Resp}(\mathsf{st},c)]=1$$

{12}------------------------------------------------

**Definition 2.13 (Special Soundness).** Let  $\mathfrak{R}$  be an NP-relation and  $\Sigma$  be an  $\Sigma$ -protocol for  $\mathfrak{R}$ . We call  $\Sigma$  (2-)special sound, if there exists a deterministic polynomial-time extractor  $\Sigma$ . Ext that given statement  $\mathbb{R}$  and two valid transcripts  $\{(A, c_b, z_b)\}_{b \in \{0,1\}}$  with  $c_0 \neq c_1$ , outputs a witness  $\mathbb{R}$  such that  $(\mathbb{R}, \mathbb{R}) \in \mathfrak{R}$ .

**Definition 2.14 (SHVZK).** Let  $\mathfrak{R}$  be an NP-relation and  $\Sigma$  be an  $\Sigma$ -protocol for  $\mathfrak{R}$ . We say that  $\Sigma$  is (perfect) special honest-verifier zero-knowledge (SHVZK), if there exists a PPT zero-knowledge simulator  $\Sigma$ . Sim such that for any (potentially unbounded) adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , it holds that for any  $(\mathfrak{x}, \mathfrak{w}) \in \mathfrak{R}$  and  $c \in \Sigma$ .  $\mathcal{C}$  that  $\mathsf{D}_{\mathsf{Real}} = \mathsf{D}_{\mathsf{SVHZK}}$  for

$$D_{\mathsf{Real}} := \{ (\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w}, (A, c, z)) \mid A \leftarrow \$ \Sigma.\mathsf{Init}(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w}); \ z \leftarrow \Sigma.\mathsf{Resp}(\mathsf{st}, c) \},$$
$$D_{\mathsf{SHVZK}} := \{ (\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w}, (A, c, z)) \mid (A, z) \leftarrow \Sigma.\mathsf{Sim}(\mathbf{x}, c) \}.$$

**Definition 2.15 ((Relaxed) Unique Responses).** Let  $\Sigma$  be a  $\Sigma$ -protocol for relation  $\mathfrak{R}$ . We say that  $\Sigma$  has unique responses if for any statement  $\mathbb{X}$  and (A,c) there exists at most one z such that (A,c,z) is valid for  $\mathbb{X}$ . We say that  $\Sigma$  has relaxed unique responses for relation  $\widetilde{\mathfrak{R}}$  if there exists an deterministic polynomial-time extractor  $\Sigma$ .URExt that given statement  $\mathbb{X}$  and two valid transcripts  $\{(A,c,z_b)\}_{b\in\{0,1\}}$  with  $z_0\neq z_1$ , outputs a witness  $\mathbb{X}$  such that  $(\mathbb{X},\mathbb{X})\in\widetilde{\mathfrak{R}}$ .

We further recall the notions of randomizable transcripts and a useful corollary from [KR25] which facilitates proving blindness.

<span id="page-12-2"></span>**Definition 2.16 (Randomizable Transcripts).** Let  $\Sigma$  be a  $\Sigma$ -protocol for relation  $\Re$  with challenge space  $\Sigma.\mathcal{C}$ , and suppose  $\Sigma$  is SHVZK. We say Rand is a randomization algorithm, if given a valid transcript (A,c,z) for  $\mathbb{X}$  the randomization  $\mathsf{Rand}(\mathbb{X},(A,c,z))$  outputs a new valid transcript for  $\mathbb{X}$ .

We say  $\Sigma$  has (perfect) randomizable transcripts (resp. strongly randomizable transcripts) if an efficient randomization algorithm Rand exists such that for all  $x \in \mathfrak{L}_{\mathfrak{R}}$  (resp. all x) and all accepting  $\pi^* = (A^*, c^*, z^*)$ , the distributions

$$D_{\mathsf{SHVZK}} := \{ (\mathbf{x}, (A, c, z)) \mid c \leftarrow \$ \ \Sigma.\mathcal{C}; \ (A, z) \leftarrow \$ \ \Sigma.\mathsf{Sim}(\mathbf{x}, c) \}$$
$$D_{\mathsf{Rand}} := \{ (\mathbf{x}, (A, c, z)) \mid (A, c, z) \leftarrow \$ \ \mathsf{Rand}(\mathbf{x}, \pi^*) \}$$

are identical.

<span id="page-12-3"></span>Corollary 2.17. Suppose  $\Sigma$  is a  $\Sigma$ -protocol for relation  $\Re$  which is SVHZK and has randomizable transcripts. Then for all  $(\mathbb{X}, \mathbb{W}) \in \Re$  and all accepting  $\pi^* = (A^*, c^*, z^*)$ , the following distributions are identical:

<span id="page-12-1"></span>
$$\begin{split} D_{\mathsf{Real}} &:= \{ (\mathbf{x}, (A, c, z)) \mid A \leftarrow \mathsf{Init}(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{w}); \ c \leftarrow \$ \ \varSigma.\mathcal{C}; \ z \leftarrow \mathsf{Resp}(\mathsf{st}, c) \} \\ D_{\mathsf{SHVZK}} &:= \{ (\mathbf{x}, (A, c, z)) \mid c \leftarrow \$ \ \varSigma.\mathcal{C}; \ (A, z) \leftarrow \varSigma.\mathsf{Sim}(\mathbf{x}, c) \} \\ D_{\mathsf{Rand}} &:= \{ (\mathbf{x}, (A, c, z)) \mid (A, c, z) \leftarrow \mathsf{Rand}(\mathbf{x}, \pi^*) \} \end{split}$$

Canonical  $\Sigma$ -Protocols Let  $\phi \colon \mathcal{W} \to \mathcal{X}$  be a  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ -linear map. We will instantiate our  $\Sigma$ -protocols with canonical  $\Sigma$ -protocols for linear maps [Mau15, KR25]. The canonical  $\Sigma$ -protocol  $\Sigma_{\phi}$  for the relation  $\mathfrak{R}_{\phi} := \{(\mathbb{X} = (\phi, T), \mathbb{W}) \mid \phi(\mathbb{W}) = T\}$  with challenge space  $\mathcal{C} = \mathbb{Z}_p$  is as follows.

- $\Sigma_{\phi}$ .Init(x, w)  $\to (A, st)$ : Sample  $r \leftarrow W$ . Output (st, A) where  $st \leftarrow (w, r)$  and  $A = \phi(r)$ .
- $\Sigma_{\phi}$ . Resp(st, c)  $\rightarrow z$ : Output  $z \leftarrow cw + r$
- $\Sigma_{\phi}$ . Ver(x, A, c, z)  $\rightarrow b$ : Check that elements are in their respective spaces of definition. <sup>3</sup> Return 1 if  $\phi(z) = A + cT$ , else 0.

This concept naturally generalizes to families of maps  $\phi_{pp}$  indexed by parameters pp. The following lemma summarizes well-known properties of  $\Sigma_{\phi}$  (cf. [KR25, Lemma 2.9]) with some additions.

<span id="page-12-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>3</sup> We will leave this step implicit in the following.

{13}------------------------------------------------

| Algorithm Fork $^{\mathcal{A}}(inp)$ :                                                                                                 | Algorithm $Fork_3^\mathcal{A}(inp)$ :                                                                                                                  |
|----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|--------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| 1: Pick random coins $\rho_{\mathcal{A}}$ ; $\boldsymbol{h} \leftarrow \mathcal{C}^{Q}$                                                | 1: Pick random coins $\rho_{\mathcal{A}}$ ; $\boldsymbol{h} \leftarrow \mathcal{C}^{Q}$                                                                |
| $2: (I, aux) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(inp, \boldsymbol{h}; \rho_{\mathcal{A}})$                                                          | $2:  ((I_0,I_1),(aux_{I_k})_{k\in\{0,1\}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(inp,\boldsymbol{h};\rho_{\mathcal{A}})$                                              |
| $3:  \mathbf{if} \ I = \bot \ \mathbf{then} \ \mathbf{return} \ \bot$                                                                  | $3:$ if $(I_0,I_1)=\bot$ then return $\bot$                                                                                                            |
| $4:  \boldsymbol{h}'_{[I:Q]} \leftarrow \mathcal{C}^{Q-I+1}; \boldsymbol{h}' = \boldsymbol{h}_{[1:I-1]} \  \boldsymbol{h}'_{[I:Q]} \ $ | 4: $h'_{[I_0:Q]} \leftarrow \mathcal{C}^{Q-I_0+1}; h' = h_{[1:I_0-1]} \  h'_{[I_0:Q]} \ $                                                              |
| $5: (I', aux') \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(inp, \boldsymbol{h}'; \rho_{\mathcal{A}})$                                                       | $5:  ((I_0',I_1'),(aux_{I_k'}')_{k\in\{0,1\}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(inp,\boldsymbol{h}';\rho_{\mathcal{A}})$                                         |
| 6: if $I \neq I' \lor h_I = h'_I$ then return $\bot$                                                                                   | 6: $\boldsymbol{h}_{[I_1:Q]}^{"} \leftarrow \mathcal{C}^{Q-I_1+1}; \boldsymbol{h}^{"} = \boldsymbol{h}_{[1:I_1-1]} \  \boldsymbol{h}_{[I_1:Q]}^{"} \ $ |
| 7: <b>return</b> $(1, aux, aux')$                                                                                                      | $7:  ((I_0'',I_1''),(aux_{I_k''}'')_{k\in\{0,1\}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(inp, \boldsymbol{h}'';\rho_{\mathcal{A}})$                                   |
|                                                                                                                                        | 8: <b>if</b> $I_0 \notin \{I'_0, I'_1\} \lor I_1 \notin \{I''_0, I''_1\} \lor$                                                                         |
|                                                                                                                                        | 9: $h_{I_0} = h'_{I_0} \vee h_{I_1} = h''_{I_1}$ then return $\perp$                                                                                   |
|                                                                                                                                        | $10:  \mathbf{return} \ (1, (aux_{I_k})_{k \in \{0,1\}}, (aux_{I_0}', aux_{I_1}''))$                                                                   |

<span id="page-13-1"></span>Fig. 4. Forking algorithms

Lemma 2.18 (Properties of  $\Sigma_{\phi}$ ). Let  $\Sigma_{\phi}$  denote the above canonical  $\Sigma$ -protocol for relation  $\mathfrak{R}_{\phi}$ . Then  $\Sigma_{\phi}$  is correct, 2-special sound, SHVZK, and has strongly randomizable transcripts. More concretely:

- Sim(x, c) samples  $z \leftarrow W$ , sets  $A \leftarrow c \cdot T \phi(z)$  and outputs (A, z).
- Rand $(\mathbf{x}, (A, c, z))$  samples  $\gamma \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p$  and  $\alpha \leftarrow \mathbb{W}$  and outputs

$$(\widetilde{A}, \widetilde{c}, \widetilde{z}) = (A - \gamma T + \phi(\alpha), c + \gamma, z + \alpha).$$

Moreover,  $\Sigma_{\phi}$  satisfies relaxed unique response for the relaxed relation  $\widetilde{\mathfrak{R}}_{\phi} := \{(\mathbf{x} = (\phi, T), \mathbf{w} \neq 0) \mid \phi(\mathbf{w}) = 0 \in \mathcal{X}\}$ , and if  $\phi$  is injective, then  $\Sigma_{\phi}$  has unique responses.

*Proof.* The statement is shown in [KR25, Lemma 2.9] except the claim concerning relaxed unique response. The latter follows immediately from inspecting the verification algorithm.

FORKING LEMMA. In our OMSUF proof, we require two variants of forking lemmas given below: the first is the version introduced by Bellare and Neven [BN06], while the second is an alternate version where the forking occurs at two indices with the proof given in Appendix B.

<span id="page-13-3"></span>**Lemma 2.19 (Forking Lemma [BN06]).** Fix an integer  $Q \ge 1$  and a set C of size  $|C| \ge 2$ . Let A be a randomized algorithm that on input inp,  $h \in C^Q$  returns a pair  $(I \in [Q] \cup \{\bot\}, \mathsf{aux})$ . Let  $\mathsf{IG}$  be a randomized algorithm for sampling inp. Let  $\mathsf{acc} := \mathsf{Pr}[I \ne \bot | \mathsf{inp} \leftarrow \mathsf{s} \mathsf{IG}; h \leftarrow \mathsf{s} C^Q; (I, \mathsf{aux}) \leftarrow \mathsf{s} A(x, h_1, \ldots, h_q)]$ . Let  $\mathsf{frk} = \mathsf{Pr}[b = 1 : \mathsf{inp} \leftarrow \mathsf{s} \mathsf{IG}; (b, \mathsf{aux}, \mathsf{aux}') \leftarrow \mathsf{s} \mathsf{Fork}^A(\mathsf{inp})]$  where  $\mathsf{Fork}^A$  is defined in Figure 4. Then,

$$\mathsf{frk} \geqslant \mathsf{acc}\left(\frac{\mathsf{acc}}{Q} - \frac{1}{|\mathcal{C}|}\right) \;, or \; alternatively, \; \mathsf{acc} \leqslant \sqrt{Q \cdot \mathsf{frk}} + \frac{Q}{|\mathcal{C}|} \;.$$

<span id="page-13-2"></span>**Lemma 2.20 (Two-Index Forking).** Fix  $Q \ge 1$ , C, and  $\mathsf{IG}$  be as in the prior lemma. Let A be a random-ized algorithm that on input inp,  $h \in C^Q$  returns a pair  $(I = (I_0, I_1) \in [Q]^2 \cup \{\bot\}, (\mathsf{aux}_{I_k})_{k \in \{0,1\}})$ . Let  $\mathsf{acc}$  still be defined as the probability that A outputs  $I \ne \bot$ . Let  $\mathsf{frk}_3 = \mathsf{Pr}[b = 1 : \mathsf{inp} \leftarrow \mathsf{s} \mathsf{IG}; (b, \mathsf{aux}, \mathsf{aux}') \leftarrow \mathsf{s} \mathsf{Fork}_3^A(\mathsf{inp})]$  where  $\mathsf{Fork}_3^A$  is defined in Figure 4. Then,

$$\mathsf{frk}_3 \geqslant \frac{\mathsf{acc}}{2} \left( \frac{\mathsf{acc}}{4Q} - \frac{1}{|\mathcal{C}|} \right)^2 \ , or \ alternatively, \ \mathsf{acc} \leqslant \sqrt[3]{32Q^2 \cdot \mathsf{frk}} + \frac{4Q}{|\mathcal{C}|}$$

### <span id="page-13-0"></span>3 Three-move Blind Signatures from Algebraic MACs

In this section, we give our three-move blind signatures construction based on pairing-free groups. We start by introducing our building blocks in Section 3.1, presenting our construction in Section 3.2, and discussing its properties in Section 3.3.

{14}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-14-0"></span>3.1 Building Blocks

LINEARLY-HOMOMORPHIC COMMITMENTS. We employ two linearly-homomorphic commitment schemes  $C_{mac}$  and  $C_{rnd}$  with message spaces  $\mathbb{Z}_p^2$  and  $\mathbb{G}^7$ , respectively. Looking ahead, we employ  $C_{mac}$  to fix parts of a MAC secret key and  $C_{rnd}$  to fix blinding randomness in the protocol. For our instantiation, we may choose packed ElGamal [BBKS07] (for compactness) or Groth-Sahai commitments [GS08] (for statistical blindness) for  $C_{rnd}$ . We discuss more on this in Section 4.

<span id="page-14-2"></span>**Algebraic MAC.** We describe the algebraic MAC we employ to tag the Okamoto-Schnorr nonces within our blind signature.

<u>DDH-BASED ALGEBRAIC MAC.</u> We rely on the DDH-based algebraic MAC scheme by Chase et al. [CMZ14], denoted  $M_{alg}$ . We give the brief description of the scheme below. Note in contrast to [CMZ14], we commit to the partial secret key  $(x_0, y_0)$  in the issuer's public parameters via  $C_{mac}$ .

 $\mathsf{M}_{\mathsf{alg}}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \to \mathsf{mpp}$ : The public parameters  $\mathsf{mpp}$  (implicitly containing the group description of  $\mathbb G$  with generator G) is  $\mathsf{mpp} = (F, \mathsf{mck})$ , where  $F \leftarrow \mathbb G$  and  $\mathsf{mck} \leftarrow \mathbb G$  and  $\mathsf{mck} \leftarrow \mathbb G$ .

 $\mathsf{M}_{\mathsf{alg}}.\mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{mpp}) \to (\mathsf{msk},\mathsf{ipp})$ : The secret key contains random scalars  $x_0, x_1, y_0, y_1, z \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p$ . The corresponding issuer's public parameters will be used in the blind signature scheme along with a proof that the tags are valid. The issuer's parameters will contain a commitment of  $(x_0, y_0)$  and the embeddings of  $x_1, y_1, z$  in the group  $\mathbb{G}$ . More precisely, we let

$$\mathsf{msk} = (x_0, x_1, y_0, y_1, z, \rho_0) \;, \qquad \mathsf{ipp} = (\mathsf{cmt}_0, X_1, Y_1, Z) \;,$$

where  $X_1 = x_1 F, Y_1 = y_1 F, Z = zF$  and  $\text{cmt}_0 = \mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{mac}}.\mathsf{Commit}(\mathsf{mck}, (x_0, y_0); \rho_0).$ 

 $\mathsf{M}_{\mathsf{alg}}.\mathsf{Tag}(\mathsf{mpp},\mathsf{msk},m) \to \sigma_{\mathsf{alg}}$ : For a message  $m \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , samples  $u \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^*$  and outputs  $\sigma_{\mathsf{alg}} = (U,V,S,T)$  with

$$U = uG$$
,  $V = zU$ ,  $S = u(x_0 + x_1 m)G$ ,  $T = u(y_0 + y_1 m)G$ .

We note that this is the version of the tagging algorithm that will be used in the OMUF security proof of our blind signature scheme.

 $\mathsf{M}_{\mathsf{alg}}.\mathsf{Ver}(\mathsf{mpp},\mathsf{ipp},\mathsf{msk},m,\sigma_{\mathsf{alg}}) \to \{0,1\}$ : To verify the tag  $\sigma_{\mathsf{alg}} = (U,V,S,T)$  of a message  $m \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , we use the secret key sk to efficiently check that

$$(U \neq 0_{\mathbb{G}}) \wedge (V = zU) \wedge (S = (x_0 + x_1 m)U) \wedge (T = (y_0 + y_1 m)U).$$

We recall the following theorem adapted from the UF-CMA security of a variant of  $M_{alg}$  without cmt<sub>0</sub> in ipp stated in [CAHLT25, Theorem 6.1.]. The additional hiding advantage is due to switching the cmt<sub>0</sub> to commit to (0,0) instead. We refer to Appendix C for the formal proof.

Theorem 3.1 (UF-RMA Security of  $M_{alg}$ ). Let GGen be a group parameters generator outputting groups of prime-order  $p = p(\lambda)$ ,  $C_{mac}$  be a commitment scheme,  $M_{alg} = M_{alg}[GGen, C_{mac}]$  be the algebraic MAC scheme described above. For any A running in time  $T_A = T_A(\lambda)$  and making at most  $Q = Q(\lambda)$  queries to the Tag oracle, there exist adversaries  $\mathcal{B}_1$  and  $\mathcal{B}_2$  running in time  $\approx T_A$  such that

<span id="page-14-1"></span>
$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ufrma}}_{\mathsf{M}_{\mathsf{alg}}}(\mathcal{A},\lambda) \leqslant \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{hide}}_{\mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{mac}}}(\mathcal{B}_1,\lambda) + Q \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ddh}}_{\mathsf{GGen}}(\mathcal{B}_2,\lambda) + \frac{3}{p} \; .$$

TAGGING ON G-MESSAGES. We describe an alternative mode to tag group elements. This mode will be used explicitly in our construction. In short, we replace mG with  $M \in \mathbb{G}$  in  $\mathsf{M}_{\mathsf{alg}}$  above. That is, for a message  $M \in \mathbb{G}$ , the tagging algorithm outputs a tag  $\sigma = (U, V, S, T)$  such that for  $u \leftarrow \mathbb{F} \mathbb{Z}_p^*$  we have

$$U = uG$$
,  $V = zU$ ,  $S = u(x_0G + x_1M)$ ,  $T = u(y_0G + y_1M)$ .

{15}------------------------------------------------

We remark that the tag cannot be efficiently verified using the secret key as u is not known to the verifier. Nevertheless, there is an underlying structure expressible in terms of a relation induced by a linear map. In particular, we have the following statement and witness

<span id="page-15-0"></span>
$$\mathbf{x}_{\text{mac}} = (\text{ipp}, M, U, V, S_0, T_0, S_1 = 0_{\mathbb{G}}, T_1 = 0_{\mathbb{G}}),$$

$$\mathbf{w}_{\text{mac}} = (u' = u^{-1}, z, x_0, x_1, y_0, y_1, \rho_0).$$

The corresponding map  $\phi_{\mathsf{mpp}, x_{\mathsf{mac}}}^{\mathsf{mac}}$  is given below. Note that  $\phi_{\mathsf{mpp}, x_{\mathsf{mac}}}^{\mathsf{mac}}$  is linear as  $\mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{mac}}$  is linearly-homomorphic.

$$\begin{pmatrix}
\operatorname{cmt}_{0} \\
X_{1} \\
Y_{1} \\
Z \\
G \\
V \\
S_{1} \\
T_{1}
\end{pmatrix} = \phi_{\mathsf{mpp}, \mathbb{m}_{\mathsf{mac}}}^{\mathsf{mac}}(\mathbb{w}_{\mathsf{mac}}) := \begin{pmatrix}
\mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{mac}}(\mathsf{mck}, (x_{0}, y_{0}); \rho_{0}) \\
x_{1} F \\
y_{1} F \\
z F \\\nu' U \\
z U \\
x_{0} G + x_{1} M - u' S_{0} \\
y_{0} G + y_{1} M - u' T_{0}
\end{pmatrix}. \tag{2}$$

RERANDOMIZING THE MAC TAGS. For blindness, we need to rerandomize the MAC tags and also hide the underlying group element message. We take similar approach as done in [CMZ14] but with modifications to accommodate the group element message. In particular, the user will compute the following group elements

$$\widetilde{U} \leftarrow \beta U$$
,  $\widetilde{V} \leftarrow \beta V$ ,  $\widetilde{M} \leftarrow M + r_M F$ ,  
 $\widetilde{S}_0 \leftarrow \beta S - r_x \widetilde{U}$ ,  $\widetilde{S}_1 \leftarrow r_x G + r_M X_1$ ,  
 $\widetilde{T}_0 \leftarrow \beta T - r_y \widetilde{U}$ ,  $\widetilde{T}_1 \leftarrow r_y G + r_M Y_1$ .

where  $\beta \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p^*, r_M, r_x, r_y \leftarrow \mathbb{Z}_p$ . Note that the following statement and witness is still in the relation induced by the map  $\phi_{\mathsf{pp},\mathbb{X}_{\mathsf{mac}}}^{\mathsf{mac}}$  such that

$$\mathbf{x}_{\mathsf{mac}} = (\mathsf{ipp}, \widetilde{M}, \widetilde{U}, \widetilde{V}, \widetilde{S}_0, \widetilde{T}_0, \widetilde{S}_1, \widetilde{T}_1) \;, \\ \mathbf{w}_{\mathsf{mac}} = (u' = u^{-1}\beta^{-1}, x_0, x_1, y_0, y_1, z, \rho_0) \;.$$

 $\Sigma$ -protocols. We will use multiple  $\Sigma$ -protocols in our construction. We assume that all these  $\Sigma$ -protocols share the following public parameters (generated by the setup of our blind signature scheme) which will be a part of the statement

$$pp = (\mathbb{G}, p, G, H, mpp, bck, D_1)$$
,

where  $\mathsf{mpp} = (F, \mathsf{mck})$  is as above in Section 3.1, and  $H \leftarrow \mathfrak{G}$ ,  $\mathsf{bck} \leftarrow \mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{rnd}}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda})$  and  $D_1 \leftarrow \mathfrak{G}$ . (By slight abuse of notation, we will pass these as input to the  $\Sigma$ -protocol algorithms for concreteness.) The commitment key  $\mathsf{bck}$  is later employed to commit to the blinding factors for the rerandomization of  $\mathsf{M}_{\mathsf{alg}}$ .

 $\underline{\Sigma\text{-PROTOCOL FOR OKAMOTO-SCHNORR.}}$  We denote by  $\Sigma_{os}$  the  $\Sigma$ -protocol underlying the Okamoto-Schnorr identification scheme [Sch90, Oka93]. The statement and witness are defined as follows

$$\mathbb{X}_{os} = (W \in \mathbb{G}),$$
  $\mathbb{W}_{os} = (w_0, w_1) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^2.$ 

The underlying relation defined by the linear map  $\phi_{\mathsf{pp}, \mathbf{x}_{\mathsf{os}}}^{\mathsf{os}}$  is

$$W = \phi_{\mathsf{pp}, x_{\mathsf{os}}}^{\mathsf{os}}(w_{\mathsf{os}}) := w_0 G + w_1 H . \tag{3}$$

We note that  $\Sigma_{os}$  satisfies relaxed unique-response for the relation  $\widetilde{\mathfrak{R}}_{os}$  corresponding to  $w \neq 0$  such that  $\phi_{\mathsf{pp}, \mathbb{x}_{os}}^{\mathsf{os}}(w) = 0_{\mathbb{G}}$ .

{16}------------------------------------------------

 $\underline{\mathcal{L}\text{-PROTOCOL FOR MAC VALIDITY.}} \text{ We denote } \mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{mac}} \text{ to be the } \mathcal{L}\text{-protocol for the relation induced by } \phi_{\mathsf{pp}, \mathbb{x}_{\mathsf{mac}}}^{\mathsf{mac}}$  given in Equation (2). We note that  $\mathcal{L}_{\mathsf{mac}}$  satisfies relaxed unique-response for the relation  $\widetilde{\mathfrak{R}}_{\mathsf{mac}}$  corresponding to witnesses  $\mathbb{w} \neq 0$  such that  $\phi_{\mathsf{pp}, \mathbb{x}_{\mathsf{mac}}}^{\mathsf{mac}}(\mathbb{w}) = \mathbf{0}_{\mathbb{G}}$ .

 $\underline{\Sigma}$ -PROTOCOL FOR EQUALITY OF DISCRETE LOGARITHMS. We denote  $\Sigma_{\mathsf{dleq}}$  as a simple  $\Sigma$ -protocol for proving membership of the discrete-logarithm equality language. The statement and witness are defined as follows

$$\mathbf{x}_{\mathsf{dleq}} = (D_1, D_2, D_3) , \qquad \mathbf{w}_{\mathsf{dleq}} = d \in \mathbb{Z}_p .$$

The underlying relation  $\mathfrak{R}_{\mathsf{dleq}}$  is induced by the linear map  $\phi_{\mathsf{pp},x_{\mathsf{dleq}}}^{\mathsf{dleq}}$  defined as

$$\begin{pmatrix} D_2 \\ D_3 \end{pmatrix} = \phi_{\mathsf{pp},D_1}^{\mathsf{dleq}}(\mathbb{W}_{\mathsf{dleq}}) := \begin{pmatrix} dG \\ dD_1 \end{pmatrix} . \tag{4}$$

We note that  $\Sigma_{\mathsf{dleq}}$  satisfies unique responses.

 $\Sigma$ -PROTOCOL FOR MAC BLINDING FACTORS. For our blind signature construction, we need the user to commit to the original nonce and their blinding factors along with proving knowledge of these blinding factors and the unblinded MAC tag. For this, the commitment cmt<sub>rnd</sub> will commit to

- the original Okamoto-Schnorr nonce  $A_{os}$ ,
- the blinding factors  $\alpha_0, \alpha_1, \gamma$  for Okamoto-Schnorr nonce, and
- the blinding factors  $r_x, r_y$  for the MACs, and
- the blinding factor  $r_M$  for the tagged message.

Formally, we define the  $\Sigma$ -protocol  $\Sigma_{rnd}$  for the relation  $\mathfrak{R}_{rnd}$  with the following statement and witness

$$\mathbf{x}_{\mathsf{rnd}} = (W, \widetilde{A}_{\mathsf{os}}, \mathsf{cmt}_{\mathsf{rnd}}, \widetilde{M}, \widetilde{U}, \widetilde{S}_{1}, \widetilde{T}_{1}) ,$$

$$\mathbf{w}_{\mathsf{rnd}} = (\rho_{\mathsf{rnd}}, \alpha_{0}, \alpha_{1}, \gamma, r_{x}, r_{y}, r_{M}) ,$$

which satisfy the identities induced by the map  $\phi_{\mathsf{pp},x_{\mathsf{rnd}}}^{\mathsf{rnd}}$  defined as follows. Let  $\boldsymbol{B} = (\alpha_0 \widetilde{U}, \alpha_1 \widetilde{U}, \gamma \widetilde{U}, r_x \widetilde{U}, r_y \widetilde{U}, r_x \widetilde{U}, r_y \widetilde{U}, \gamma \widetilde{U}, \gamma W - \alpha_0 G - \alpha_1 H)$  be a placeholder for the committed values (and is a linear function over the witness) and  $\mathsf{cmt}' = \mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{rnd}}.\mathsf{Commit}(\mathsf{ck}, (0_{\mathbb{G}}, \dots, 0_{\mathbb{G}}, \widetilde{A}_{\mathsf{os}}); 0)$ , and let

$$\begin{pmatrix}
\operatorname{cmt}_{\mathsf{rnd}} - \operatorname{cmt}' \\
\widetilde{M} - \widetilde{A}_{\mathsf{os}} \\
\widetilde{S}_{1} \\
\widetilde{T}_{1}
\end{pmatrix} = \phi_{\mathsf{pp}, \mathbb{X}_{\mathsf{rnd}}}^{\mathsf{rnd}}(\mathbb{W}_{\mathsf{rnd}}) := \begin{pmatrix}
\mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{rnd}}.\mathsf{Commit}(\mathsf{ck}, \boldsymbol{B}; \rho_{\mathsf{rnd}}) \\
r_{M}F - \alpha_{0}G - \alpha_{1}H + \gamma W \\
r_{x}G + r_{M}X_{1} \\
r_{y}G + r_{M}Y_{1}
\end{pmatrix}.$$
(5)

The equality on the first entry shows that  $\operatorname{cmt}_{\mathsf{rnd}}$  commits to the blinding factors and  $A_{\mathsf{os}} = \widetilde{A}_{\mathsf{os}} - \alpha_0 G - \alpha_1 H + \gamma W$ . The second one shows that  $\widetilde{M} = \widetilde{A}_{\mathsf{os}} - \alpha_0 G - \alpha_1 H + \gamma W + r_M F = A_{\mathsf{os}} + r_M F$ . For the third and fourth entries, the equations show that  $\widetilde{S}_1, \widetilde{T}_1$  are computed with respect to the blinding factors  $r_x, r_y, r_M$ . We also note that  $\Sigma_{\mathsf{rnd}}$  satisfies relaxed unique response for the following relaxed relation  $\widetilde{\mathfrak{R}}_{\mathsf{rnd}}$  containing witnesses  $w \neq 0 \in \mathsf{C}_{\mathsf{rnd}}.\mathcal{R} \times \mathbb{Z}_p^6$  such that  $\phi^{\mathsf{rnd}}_{\mathsf{pp},x_{\mathsf{rnd}}}(w) = 0$ .

<span id="page-16-0"></span>Remark 3.2 (Interplay of  $\Sigma_{\mathsf{mac}}$  and  $\Sigma_{\mathsf{rnd}}$ ). We note that given a blinded  $\mathsf{M}_{\mathsf{alg}}$  tag  $\widetilde{\sigma}_{\mathsf{mac}} = (\widetilde{U}, \widetilde{V}, \widetilde{S}_0, \widetilde{T}_0, \widetilde{S}_1, \widetilde{T}_1)$  and  $\widetilde{M}$  such that (1) along with ipp, the tuple (ipp,  $\widetilde{M}, \widetilde{\sigma}$ ) is in the language induced by  $\phi_{\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{x}_{\mathsf{mac}}}^{\mathsf{mac}}$ , and (2) when additionally given the witness  $\mathsf{w}_{\mathsf{rnd}} = (\rho_{\mathsf{rnd}}, \alpha_0, \alpha_1, \gamma, r_x, r_y, r_M)$  of the relation induced by  $\phi_{\mathsf{pp}, \mathsf{x}_{\mathsf{rnd}}}^{\mathsf{rnd}}$ , one can recover a valid MAC tag  $\sigma_{\mathsf{mac}}^* = (\widetilde{U}, \widetilde{V}, \widetilde{S}_0 + r_x \widetilde{U}, \widetilde{T}_0 + r_y \widetilde{U})$  for  $m^* = \mathsf{dlog}_G A_{\mathsf{os}} = \mathsf{dlog}_G \widetilde{A}_{\mathsf{os}} - \alpha_0 - \alpha_1 \mathsf{dlog}_G H + \gamma_{\mathsf{os}} \mathsf{dlog}_G W$ . In our security analysis we will leverage MAC security in one step (cf. Case 2 in Section 3.3). In that case, the elements H and W will be generated with known discrete logarithms, so the reduction can recover the discrete logarithm of  $\widetilde{A}_{\mathsf{os}}$  from the  $\Sigma_{\mathsf{os}}$  transcript. This allows to employ MAC security for  $\mathbb{Z}_p$  elements.

{17}------------------------------------------------

            W \leftarrow w_0 G + w_1 H

 5:

            \mathsf{sk} \leftarrow (\mathsf{msk}, s) \; ; \mathsf{pk} \leftarrow (\mathsf{ipp}, W)
 6:
